3.13   Civil Works. All contracts for highway construction and rehabilitation, and bridge
construction to be financed by the Bank and expected to be valued at more than US$3 million and
the contract for road signalization will be procured on the basis of ICB procedures and in
accordance with the "Bank Standard Documents for Procurement of Works (Smaller Contracts),
January 1995".
3.14 Civil works for highway resurfacing and routine maintenance for contract between US$3
million and 250,000 will be awarded under National Competitive Bidding (NCB) which will be
based on standard bidding documents to be approved by the Bank (national competitive bidding
procedures are acceptable for highway resurfacing and routine maintenance by contract because
2Price quotations.
3 Consulting services selected in accordance with World Bank Guidelines for "Use of Consultants by World Bank
Borrowers and by the World Bank as Executing Agency (August 1981)."



- 26 -
the works are small -- none will cost more than US$0.5 million -- and are unlikely to attract
international bidders).
3.15  Routine maintenance works estimated to cost less than US$250,000 equivalent per
contract will be procured on the basis of price quotations obtained from three qualified local
contractors (with experience and resources to successfully complete the contract) in response to a
written invitation. The invitation will include a detailed description of the works, including basic
specifications, the required completion date, a basic form of agreement acceptable to the Bank
and relevant drawings when necessary. The award will be made to the contractor who offers the
lower price quotation for the required work.
3.16 Civil works of US$ 10 million and more will be procured on the basis of ICB procedures in
accordance with "Bank Standard Documents for Procurement of Works (Major Works), January
1995". Prequalification of civil works contractors for contracts of US$10 million and more is
essential to the bidding process and will be based on "Prequalification Procurement of Works, April
1993 and Bidding Documents Procurement of Works, January 1995" and defined in terms of
minimum working capital, professional experience, volume and scope of similar works. All
contractors (foreign and local) interested in being prequalified will be evaluated on the basis of "fail
or pass evaluation criteria"; that is, only those complying with the minimum criteria will be
qualified.
3.17 Goods. Vehicles, spare parts and office equipment will be purchased in accordance with
the Bank's "Standard Bidding Documents. Procurement of Goods, The World Bank, January
1995." Goods estimated to cost less than US$250,000 per contract may be awarded through
NCB, using standard bidding documents acceptable to the Bank, except for items costing less
than US$50,000, which could be purchased using national or international shopping.
3.18  Consulting Services. Consultants for civil work supervision, technical assistance, training
programs, and auditing services will be selected and engaged following the "Guidelines for the
Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by The World Bank as Executing Agency,
August 1981" using the Bank's "Standard Form of Contract for Consultants' Services for Time-
based Complex Assignments and for Lump Sum Remuneration, June 1995."
3.19 Procurement Review. The Bank will review ex-ante all procurement documentation
(advertising, bidding documents, bid evaluation and contract award) under ICB as well as the first
two contracts in each year under NCB. The documents for the remaining contracts under NBC
will be subject to ex-post review by the Bank. All other procurement documentation will be
retained by the SEOPC and subject to selective ex-post review by the Bank's supervision
missions. The provisions of the Consultant Guidelines requiring prior Bank review or approval of
budgets, short lists, selection procedures, letters of invitations, proposals, evaluation reports and
contracts shall not apply to: (a) contracts for the employment of consulting firms estimated to cost
US$100,000 equivalent or less each; or (b) contracts for the employment of individual consultants
estimated to cost $50,000 equivalent or less each. However, this exemption to prior Bank review
will not apply to: (a) terms of reference for such contracts; (b) single source selection of firms; (c)
assignments of a critical nature as reasonably determined by the Bank; (d) amendments to
contracts for the employment of consulting firms raising the contract value to $100,000 equivalent



- 27 -
or more; or (e) amendments to contracts for the employment of individual consultants raising the
contract value to $50,000 equivalent or more. Under these procedures, Bank's prior review will
cover about 80% of the total contract values.
3.20 Advance Procurement Action and Retroactive Financing. The loan could finance
retroactively expenditures incurred, inter alia, under the highway resurfacing program and the
pilot program of routine maintenance by contract as well as for technical assistance to support
the implementation of those programs. The implementation of those components would be done
in consultation with the Bank, including a review by the Bank of all procurement actions.

90% of other items procured locally for purchase of vehicles, spare parts and office equipment;
(e) 60% of total expenditures for civil work supervision and technical audits; and, (f) 80% of the

3.23   Special Account and Local Project Account. A Special Account will be opened and
maintained in US dollars in the Central Bank with an initial deposit of US$3 million rising to
US$5 million after withdrawals and commitments reach US$12 million. The Bank will replenish
the Special Account upon receipt of disbursement requests from the Borrower. DARE, with the
assistance of the PCT, will control the use of the Special Account and be responsible for
preparing the disbursement requests on behalf of the Borrower. These requests should be
submitted every month, or when the Special Account has been drawn to 35% of its initial deposit,
whichever comes first. In addition, not later than thirty days after the Effective Date, the
Borrower will open and thereafter maintain until completion of the project a deposit account in
Dominican pesos, for purposes of managing the counterpart funds, and make an initial deposit
equivalent to US$1.5 million or about four months of local contribution. The operating
arrangements for the Special Account and the Local Account were agreed at negotiations and are
reflected in the Agreed Minutes of Negotiations, signed with the Borrower.
3.24   Statement of Expenditures and Retroactive Financing. Disbursement requests in
respect of civil work contracts over US$3.0 million, acquisition of goods over US$250,000 and
consultant contracts over US$100,000 and US$50,000 respectively (para. 3.19) will be fully
documented. In all other cases, disbursements will be made against statements of expenditure
(SOEs). All SOEs will be transmitted through DARE, which will retain the supporting
documentation in separate files for each SOE and make it available for examination by Bank staff
during supervision missions (Annex 13). During project preparation, it was confirmed that there
are sufficient and qualified staff in DARE to record, handle and report on the SOE related



- 29 -
transactions. Retroactive financing is recommended for payments made under the project after
February 15, 1996, but before the date of loan signature, up to a total of US$6.0 million to
finance, inter alia, the highway resurfacing program, the pilot program of routine maintenance by
contract, and technical assistance to the DGM and DARE (para. 3.20). The amount to be
financed retroactively with loan proceeds was confirmed at negotiations.
3.25 Accounting. The Borrower will have accounting and control systems capable of reliably
recording and reporting all financial transactions. To this effect, the appraisal mission evaluated
the accounting and financial system in force at DARE and discussed the new accounting system in
implementation by independent consultants financed by the IDB. The new financial management
system (accounting and reporting system) will be fully operative before project effectiveness and
will cover the following aspects: i) system of budgeting and accounting, including accounting
standards used; ii) internal control system and procedures; iii) flow of funds to support the project
organization and the project (including Bank funds); iv) financial reporting arrangements,
including linkages to key performance indicators; and v) auditing arrangements. This review will
be followed by specific recommendations to guarantee that the designs of the financial
management and accounting structure directly related to the proposed project, including staffing,
is satisfactory.
3.26  Audits. All records relating to project expenditures will be kept by the project
administration unit within DARE. Based on previous experience, the appraisal mission fully
documented the accounting staff of DARE and the external auditors on the scope and meaning of
the Bank's audit standards and financial information requirements. The scope and content of the
following audit reports were extensively discussed: Statement of Sources and Application of
Funds and Investments in the project; Supplementary Financial Information on the project; the
Statements of Expenditures --SOEs; the Special Account; the evaluation of the internal control
environment, and management's integrity and underlying ethical values; the "compliance opinions"
and the "management letter". This scope and content is shown in Annex 14 jointly with
instructions regarding the selection of the firm, working standards, scheduling of reports,
concurrence of the reports, guidelines for the preparation of the Audit service contract and terms
of reference for the preparation of the audit. Certified copies of the audit reports will be furnished
to the Bank within six months after the end of each fiscal year. The audit report will include a
separate opinion by the auditor on disbursements against certified SOEs. The amount for
retroactive financing would be included in the annual audit of the fiscal year when the loan
agreement is signed. In addition to the "financial-administrative-compliance audit", an annual
"engineering audit" (technical audit) has been provided as detailed in paragraphs 1.29 and 3.7.
PROJECT BENEFITS
3.27 The improvement of the highway network (Table 3.5) and the elimination of the main
existing bottlenecks will reduce congestion, accidents and vehicle operating costs, thereby
creating incentives for tourism promotion and increased production, and reducing outlays of
scarce foreign exchange. However, the benefits from this improvement will not be maintained in
the long term without assuring a well executed maintenance program for the highway network
and the setting up of an efficient organization to plan and carry out this maintenance program; this
will be a major contribution of Bank's participation in the proposed project. The necessary



- 30 -
changes in reorganization of the work force; the methodologies introduced under Loan 3350-DO
in maintenance management; the introduction of routine maintenance by contract; and, the
consolidation of the use of new technologies in road resurfacing, also introduced under the same
loan, will be implemented only with Bank involvement and close supervision.

million to cover about four months of local contribution (see para. 3.23); (b) inadequate
maintenance execution. To minimize this risk, the project will finance a program of highway
resurfacing and a pilot program of routine maintenance, both to be contracted out; in addition, the
SEOPC maintenance organization will be strengthened; (c) insufficient maintenance financing
after the project. To minimize this risk, SEOPC will establish a Road Fund for road and bridge
maintenance. Risks (a), (b), and (c) have been analyzed to quantify the impacts on the economic
worthiness of subprojects (see paras. 2.21 and 2.22 and Annex 8). They result in a reduction of
the benefits of the project but, even under those circumstances, all sub-projects show positive
NPVs; (d) the proposed Road Fund is not implemented before project completion. This risk is
manageable, because risks associated with failure to establish the fund do not threaten project
implementation directly as the financing of the project does not require the proceeds from the
Road Fund. Nevertheless, the Fund will help achieve the sustainability of the projects objectives
and its concept and rationale have already taken hold among SEOPC's management. The SEOPC
will adopt the instruments necessary to establish the Fund by December 31, 1998 (see para. 2.12
and Table 3.1); (e) high staff turnover, motivated by the low salaries paid by the SEOPC or
political events. To prevent high staff turnover, as a transitory arrangement until the new road
agency (ONMV) is established, the SEOPC will establish a Project Coordinating Team and
continue to allow its skilled staff to take outside employment after working hours as a supplement
to their salaries. With respect to political changes, since 1988 there have been no substantial
turnovers and after the elections held in May 1994 and 1996 only the staff politically involved has
been removed.



- 31 -
4. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
4.1   During negotiations, agreement was reached on the following:
(a)   as conditions of project effectiveness,
(i)   the new financial management system for SEOPC will be established and in
operation in a manner satisfactory to the Bank (para. 3.25);
(ii)  the Borrower will establish the PCT and employ the Project Coordinating
Team listed in Annex 9 (para. 3.1);
(iii)  the SEOPC will adopt the Environmental Manual for Road Design and
Construction (para. 2.7);
(b)   not later than September 30 of each year, the SEOPC will prepare and furnish to
the Bank a satisfactory Highway Investment and Maintenance Program (HMP)
and, not later than March 31 of each year, will prepare and furnish to the Bank a
report with respect to, inter alia, the amount of budgeted funds committed and/or
used in carrying out the HIMP for the preceding fiscal year (para. 2.6.a);
(c)   in the event that population resettlement is needed to carry out any subproject, the
Borrower shall not later than the date in which it furnishes to the Bank the
corresponding Highway Investment and Maintenance Program in which such
subproject is included: (i) prepare and furnish to the Bank a resettlement plan for
such population satisfactory to the Bank; and (ii) thereafter carry out such plan in
accordance with its terms (para. 2.7.a);
(d)   in the event that the final engineering designs of a subproject require acquisition of
land therefor, the Borrower not later than the date in which it furnishes to the
Bank the corresponding Highway Investment and Maintenance Program in which
such subproject is included, shall: (a) prepare and furnish to the Bank, a land
acquisition plan, satisfactory to the Bank; and (b) thereafter carry out such plan in
accordance with its terms (para 2.7.b);
(e)   the SEOPC will prepare and initiate action for the adoption of the instruments
necessary to establish a road fund and a new road agency (ONMV) not later than
December 31, 1998 (para. 2.12);
(f)   the SEOPC will consult with the Bank before contracting any work under the
project if final and original designs of subprojects resulted in cost estimates
exceeding those of the preliminary engineering designs by more than 25% in US
dollar equivalent (paras. 2.17 and 2.20);
(g)   the SEOPC will employ and thereafter retain, until completion of the project, a
transport specialist (with qualifications and experience satisfactory to the Bank) to



-32 -
assist the SEOPC in the preparation of transport programs, not later than
December 31, 1997 (para. 3.1);
(h)   the SEOPC will prepare and furnish to the Bank by March 31, 1997, with the
assistance of consultants, terms and guidelines acceptable to the Bank to carry out
the program of routine maintenance by contract (para. 3.4);
(i)   not later than thirty days after the Effective Date, the Borrower will open, and
thereafter maintain until completion of the project, a deposit account, under terms
and conditions satisfactory to the Bank, for purposes of depositing the counterpart
funds and make an initial deposit of US$ 1,500,000 equivalent in that account
(para. 3.23); and
Gj)   the Borrower will comply with the agreed action program for institutional
development (Table 3.1).
4.2   Recommendation. With the above agreements, the project is suitable for a Bank fixed-
rate single currency loan of US$ 75 million, with standard amortization term, grace period and
interest rate and an expected disbursement period of 3-6 years.

ANNEX I
DOMINICAN REPUBLIC
NATIONAL HIGHWAY PROJECT
SEOPC's ADMINISTRATIVE SYSTEM AND FINANCIAL MANAGEMENT STRUCTURE
A.   Administrative System
1.    The administrative system at SEOPC is highly centralized and congested. The
overcentralized power and decision making structure at Secretary level is just the reflect of the
global system of administration prevailing at the country's public level, according to a "Diagnostic
on the Central Financial Organization and Plan for the Development of the Quality and Timing of
the Accounting-Finance Information of the SEOPC" ("the Diagnostic"), presented by Horwarth
Consulting in January 1995.
2.    The current centralized organization goes against the necessary delegation of the
administrative processes toward the lower, hierarchical levels (departments and divisions) and the
Districts. These, however, should be strengthened before any substantive delegation or
administrative decentralization is decided.
3.    The institutional growth and functions diversification of the SEOPC has not run in
parallel, however, to a similar trend in the legal and normative framework. It is necessary the
harmonization of the laws and the issuing of the related regulations which allow for the proper
functioning of the laws and the introduction of structures and systems to streamline the
administration and functional processes of the SEOPC.
4.    During project appraisal, it was noticed that there is no a definitive institutional
organigram in force at the SEOPC; neither the existing organigram is known to all General
Directorates. As a result, there are certain key officers unaware of which units reporting to him
are or to where they should report. The secrecy in distributing the official "organization chart"
has resulted in several cases in a free interpretation of the new reporting and dependency lines. It
is necessary that a single organigram be produced, approved by the Secretary and distributed
among all the functional units jointly with the respective Functions Manual.
B.    Financial Management Structure
5.    The basic financial management center at the SEOPC is, conventionally, the General
Directorate of Administration (DGA). The DGA accounts for the three basic functional units:
Accounting, Budget and Treasury.
6.    Neither the Budget or the Treasury fulfill a proper financial activity or are used by
management as managerial control tools or for the preparation of financial projection and
disbursement programs.



-36 -
7.    The SEOPC accounting system is totally manual and the accounting process is extremely
slow. The system does not cover some basic items, such as inventories which, until 1994, were
handled as expenses rather than as tangible assets. The accounting system in force is not devised
to produce a regular and reliable flow of management information.
8.    The Accounting Department at the DGA is not the only accounting unit within the
SEOPC. Therefore, it cannot be considered, as it should, the central recording system. The
Administrative Department of External Resources - DARE, created in order to keep record of the
financial resources from international lending, maintains its own separated accounting system and
the Directorate for Maintenance of Highways and Rural Roads (DGM) keeps also an accounting
unit. So, a sense of accounting indiscipline is noted, derived from the existing autonomy and
disconnection at various levels of the organization. This situation is not conducive to the
maintenance of controls and reliable information for management.
9.    In some units, the operations are registered focusing on the programs utilizing computer
terminals connected to the computer center. But the resulting reports are not reconciled with
those kept in the Central Accounting Department. Such is the case of the inventory at the Central
Storage, the fixed assets accounts, the complementary payroll, the issuing of checks by the
Treasury and the transactions from international resources which are kept separated at DARE.
Since no formal liaison reporting has been established between these individual units and the
Central Accounting Department, the information which this Unit should produce for SEOPC is,
necessarily, incomplete.
10.   The SEOPC, as an institutional entity, needs a uniform accounting system where the
individual contributions from the different functional units be summarized and reported -- such is
the case of DARE. That implies the introduction of a "system by projects" or "principal
activities" where while recognizing the Government budgetary systems, integrate the different
sub-accounting centers and records into the Central Accounting of SEOPC. The complete
computerization of the whole accounting system is definitely a need. The new accounting system
should be based on a new Chart of Accounts and the whole package should produce an
Accounting Plan and Financial Information System.
11.   The whole concept of "financial reporting" at SEOPC should be reviewed. The financial
execution by projects; keeping of special accounts (such as those needed for reviewing and
processing bill of quantities); the form, content and distribution of the reports, should be
revamped. The establishrnent of a timely "management information system" should be the
ultimate product of the recommended computerized accounting system.
12.   The Budget Unit is theoretically the coordination center of the budget assigned to the
SEOPC programs. The keeping of the budget, however, does not allow for the comparison
between the execution and the accounting records. In fact, there is no formal communication
system established for the necessary interchange of information between the Budget and the
Accounting Units. Under the circumstances, it is not possible to establish a "budgetary control"
and the execution of the budget is divorced from the accounting and treasury functions.



- 37 -
13.   The Treasury unit work consists, basically, of a "payment of checks window" and no
financial planning or projections of funds are prepared as a decision making element for the
management.
14.   During project appraisal, "the Diagnostic" was evaluated and considered a fair account of
the financial management picture at the SEOPC. Therefore, it is recommended that the SEOPC
management take the necessary actions in order to design and implement the recommendations
and conclusions of the consultants. However, it was realized that, except for the incidence in
DARE, the current financial management situation regarding the accounting, budgetary and
Treasury functions bear no direct effect on the current or the under study operation. The above
comments have been included only for the Bank to have a reminder, in any eventual Government
Reform Program, of the situation in this important area of the SEOPC. At any event in the
process of the proposed project the SEOPC's management would be warned about the financial
management condition and urge them to take a corrective action.
15.   In the area of control the SEOPC has the Units of "Controllership" (Contraloria) and
Internal Audit. In addition, the "Supervision and Fiscalization of Works Directorate" is in charge
of technical control. Up to the beginning of the current administration it was a "Controllership
and Internal Audit" Unit reporting directly to the Secretary of State. Based partially on the
reconmmendations of the "Diagnostic", this Unit was split in two: "Controllership" and "Internal
Audit". As a result of the project appraisal mission, the recommendations shown in the two
following paragraphs were adopted.
16.   The "Controllership Unit", consisting of a single officer, with no control function,
technically speaking, was eliminated.
17.   The Internal Audit Unit's (which used to report to the "Controller") organization,
functions, standards, apparent quality of the staff, reports produced and its distribution is far away
from what is considered currently the work of a true internal audit function in a modern
Government. The staff is inexperienced and in extreme need of training (the low level of salaries
has been one of the reasons for the departure of senior and qualified staff). The internal audit
reports are not prepared following professional working standards nor audit procedures. The
appraisal mission prepared the attached (see attached Appendix) "Suggested Organization"
"Profile of the Internal Performance Auditor" and "Terms of Reference for the new Internal
Performance Audit Unit".



- 38 -
APPENDIX
TO ANNEX I
Suggested Organization for a
"General Directorate of Internal Performance Audit"
L Concept, Sbucture and Standards
Concept of Internal Performance Auditing (EPA)
1.1    The Internal Performance Auditing (IPA) is an independent, "ex-post" appraisal function to
be established within the Secretariat of Public Works and Communications (SEOPC). To this
effect, SEOPC should structure a Internal Performance Audit Office (IAO) to be headed
by a Internal Performance Auditor and staffed with a multidisciplinary professional nix
compatible with the nature of the activities and programs of SEOPC.
1.2    The denomination "Internal Performance Audit" clearly shows the current trends in
Govemment's intemal auditing. In effect, the work of the IPA should go beyond the
administrative-accounting-financial aspects, to cover as well the value-for-money or
economy-efficiency-effectiveness of the SEOPC's activities and programs. It should cover
also the assessment of the work being done intemally with respect to the environment-related
financial issues, impacts, compliance and evaluations. That is, the intemal audit function
embraces the total integrateat global performance of the SEOPC.
1.3    The objective of the IAO should be to furnish the SEOPC with analysis, appraisals,
recommendations and counsel, on the SEOPC's operations, programs, projects, activities and
functions.
1.4    The function of the IAO is of a prevenfive and advisory nature. However, it would closely
monitor the achievement of the institutional objectives andgoals and thefinancial,
administrative, institutional, and- when applicable - environmentalperformance of the
SEOPC's operations and programs.
1.5    Consistent with the current international understanding of the intemal audit function, this
should be exercised, as indicated in paragraph 1.1, on an "ex post" perspective; that is, it
should not be the responsibility of the IPA to pre-control orpre-approve any of the
operations or transactions whose processing is the responsibility of the regular administrative
and operational units of the organization.
1.6    The Internal Performance Audit function is independent (i) in the sense that the Internal
Perforrnance Auditor, although administratively reporting to the head of SEOPC, still must be
independent.from the functions subject to his/her examinations and evaluations, and (ii)
because he/she must use independence of criterion while exercising such functions.



- 39 -
IL ProfleoftheinternalPerformanceAuditor (PA)
2.1     The individuals to be engaged in the Internal Performance Audit activities should represent,
as needed, a multidisciplinway professional origin: accounting, business adninistration or
management; and engineering, law, economics, environmental sciences, and, when justified,
social sciences.
2.2     The LAO shall be headed by a professional with proven experience in financial management,
accountingand auditing the Internal Performance Auditor (IPA).
2.3    In order to be eligible for the IPA position the candidate must have a University background
in the fields of: Accounting, Finance, Administration, Engineering or Auditing. Other
University graduates (in Economics, Law, etc.) could also be considered as long as they have
working experience in the broad area offinancial management, preferably in the public
sector. Specific training in modern internal auditing can be obtained through "ad hoc" crash
programs of study. In addition, the candidate should have not less than five years of
combined professional experience in the private and public sectors in any of the above
mentioned fields.
2.4     The candidate should be endowed with professional integrity and honesty.
2.5     The candidate should be subject to the necessaty updating/training in the areas of knowledge
required for the proper discharge of his/her responsibilities.
llL Terms of Reference and Basic Working Program
3.1     To utilize, as applicable, the audit procedures and approach recommended in the Standards
for the Professional Practice ofInternal Auditing issued by the (Intemational) Institute of
Internal Auditors.
3.2     To prepare at the beginning of each fiscal year aProgrwn ofActivities oriented toward the (i)
prevention of administrative and financial shortenings, (ii) advise to management and other
mernbers of SEOPC, and (iii) continuous monitoring of the performance of the entity. This
program will be discussed and approved by the Secretary of State.
3.3     To check the efficiency of the accounting, financial and operational internal control system
structure and environment.
3.4     To examine SEOPC's institutional budget performance, including the cost variations or
deviations and the related reports on the execution.
3.5     To perform the financial-operational-environmental examination, embracing (i) the
verification of the existence - of SEOPC's assets, liabilities, and results, including the
liabilities and costs associated to the environment-related transactions or projects, and (ii) the
inspection visits to selected offices and work sites where the major, medium and small size
projects are being executed by SEOPC.



- 40 -
3.6     To appraise the economy and efficiency with which SEOPC employees the human, financial,
physical, and when applicable, environmental resources of SEOPC.
3.7     To follow the effectiveness and accomplishment of established objectives and goals for
SEOPC's operational programs and projects; and to analyze and interpret the pre-established
performance indcators to measure the results of SEOPC.
3.8     To evaluate, when applicable, the risks associated with environmental-related activities and
official regulations. To this effect, it will focus on SEOPC's structure of responsibilities and
policies, practices, procedures, processes and resources for protecting the environment and
managing environmental issues; that is, the SEOPC's Environmental and Management
Systems - EMS.
3.9     To review the reliability and integrity of financial and operational information and the means
used, within the SEOPC, to identify, measure, classify, and report such information.
3.10    To design and monitor the administrative systems andprocedures aimed at the prevention of
waste, uneconomic practices and illegal acts during the execution of the SEOPC's activities.
3.11    To be instrumental in helping to evaluate the integrity of the entity's performance and ethical
compliance efforts of the SEOPC, especially those activities that relate to the wide spectrum
represented by financial management, accounting and control practices.
3.12    To assess the systems established to ensure compliance, by all SEOPC's departments, with
the operationalpolicies, plans and procedures of the organization, including those related to
the protection of the environment; and to report if the entity is in compliance.
3.13    To observe the compliance, by all departments of SEOPC, with the laws and regulafions
affecting SEOPC's activities, including those aimed at the protection of the environment; and
to report if the entity is in compliance.
3.14    To prepare, as needed, compliance auditing reports, financial reports, economy and
efficiency reports, and program effectiveness reports.
3.15    To consult with the SEOPC's Legal Counsel regarding preferred environmental activities,
including (i) the responsibilities attached to such activities; (ii) the preferred audit approach
and procedures, and (iii) any documentation and record retention policy related to the
environmental audits.
3.16    To assist or provide technical support as needed to the SEOPC's DARE in the preparation of
the external audit arrangements; and to cooperate with the extemal auditors - as may be
mutually agreed - in the planning and execution of the annual exaniination.



- 41 -
3.17    To follow up the actions taken by the managers of the SEOPC on the recommendations
presented in the audit reports after they were accepted by the respective managers; or find out
the reasons for not having taken them.

ANNEX 2
DOMINICAN REPUBLIC
NATIONAL HIGHWAY PROJECT
Government of the Dominican Republic
Secretariat of Public Works and Communications (SEOPC)
POLICY DECLARATION FOR THE TRANSPORT SECTOR
1.    The Government of the Dominican Republic will, through the proposed National
Highways Project, engage a consultant to assist SEOPC in developing a transport sector
policy. In the interim, the government's current policy and strategy in the sector are
presented below:
A.    General Transport Policy
2.    The Government's policy in the transport sector is aimed at:
(a) increasing competition among the various operators in the sector;
(b) fostering in the participation of the private sector both in investments and the
administration and operation of services;
(c) modernizing, repairing and maintaining the country's deteriorated transport
infrastructure;
(d) increasing the efficiency of the sector's agencies;
(e) ensuring adequate cost recovery in the sector; and
(f) integrating environmental impact and mitigatory measures more closely in the
planning, design, construction and operation of the sector's facilities.
B.    Increased Competition
3.    The Government's policy on competition in the sector is to foster unimpeded
access and competition. To that end, the Government will review the regulatory
mechanisms presently in place and modify them, as necessary, to achieve that policy
objective.
C.    Private Sector Participation
4.    Government's policy envisages the participation of the private sector in financing
transport facilities which would then be operated under concession contracts with the
private sector. This includes investments in both port and airport improvements as well as
in major roads to be operated as toll roads. With regard to operating transport facilities,
private sector participation is already a reality. Practically all the country's ports and



- 44 -
airports operate under some type of concession arrangement for cargo and passenger
handling and for maintenance activities. For ports, the Government, under a proposed
loan being discussed with the 1DB, plans to redefine the role of the Dominican Port
Authority to be normative while the private sector increases its participation in those
services in which it has demonstrated a greater efficiency and effectiveness. For civil
aviation, the Government is holding discussions with various interested parties for the
operation of the routes of the national carrier, the Dominican Aviation Company. For
roads, the Government already uses the private sector for all road construction and
rehabilitation and for periodic maintenance. Under the proposed National Roads Project
being discussed with the he World Bank, the Government will embark on a program of
routine maintenance by private contractors.
5.    Despite the important investments made recently in the sector, much work remains
to be done in view of the increased transport demand and the changing base of the
economy with increased contribution from tourism and free zones. In order to permit the
transport sector to play its role adequately, the Government has embarked on a program
to modernize the facilities, rehabilitate deteriorated infrastructure and continue to establish
effective maintenance systems to prevent further deterioration. In the planning of the
resources to be spent on these activities, the Government uses economic, financial,
technical and environmental criteria accepted world-wide.
6.    Modernization: For ports, a proposed loan from the IDB is aimed, inter alia, at
assisting in modernizing the facilities at the country's major ports. For highways, the
proposed National Highways Project, to be financed by the World Bank, would assist in
widening high-traffic main roads which have exceeded their and replace major bridges
which have either reached the end of their economic lives or their load bearing capacity.
For urban roads, a major study is under way to determine the scope and cost of activities
required, upgrade urban road infrastructure, improve mass transit service and modernize
traffic regulation.
7.    Repair and Maintenance: The Government intends to rehabilitate the major
ports as part of its concession strategy. Their subsequent maintenance would be carried
out as part of the concession by the private sector. For roads, ongoing projects with the
World Bank and the IDB are already helping to repair the main network and feeder roads
respectively. The proposed World Bank project would assist in continuing this effort. In
the case of roads, the Government expects that the increased involvement of the private
contractors in carrying out routine maintenance would lead to higher efficiency. Also,
SEOPC's ongoing pilot program on the use of cold mixes for resurfacing is expected to
reduce the cost of maintenance and permit wider coverage with maintenance funds.
E.    Increasing the Efficiency of the Sector's Agencies
8.    A major objective of the Government is to strengthen the sector's institutions to
effectively discharge their duties. The Government is fully aware that inefficient
institutions play as much role as insufficient funding in the sector's ineffectiveness. The



- 45 -
Government will take the necessary steps under the Proposed National Roads Project to
improve the coordination of investment planning and policy formulation among the
sector's agencies by consolidating that responsibility, de facto as well as de jure, within the
SEOPC. A consultant would be hired under the project to assist the SEOPC to formulate
an overall transport sector policy. SEOPC's Planning Department would also be
strengthen through the recruitment of one transport economist and a consulting firm to
assist in coordinating and consolidating the investment plans of the various transport
sector agencies.
9.    The government has identified the need in the ports sub-sector to separate out the
normative functions of planning, setting standards, regulating port activities and
formulating policy from operating and maintaining port facilities. The Government has
adopted, as part of its discussions with the IDB, a plan to assign the normative functions
to the Dominican Port authority and to leave the operations and maintenance to the
private sector in the form of concessions.
10.  In the roads sub-sector, the Government will continue the institutional
strengthening of the SEOPC, especially in planning maintenance and in managing
contracted maintenance. The Government will also improve the capacity of SEOPC's
Planning Department to coordinate overall sector planning and policy formulation.
F.    Ensuring adequate Cost Recovery in the Sector
11.   Ports, airports and road-user charges currently in force adequately cover the
marginal costs for each of the sub-sectors. However, in both ports and airports current
charges make no allowance for depreciation of assets. In the increasing use of
concessions in port and airport operations and maintenance, the Government plans to
allow for such depreciation in its costing of the concessions. In the case of roads, it is
Government's policy to adjust road-user charges to at least cover the maintenance and the
administration of the network.
G.   Environmental Impact and Mitigation in Transport
12.   The Government is committed to environmentally sustainable development. To
this end, the Government's policy is to integrate into the planning, design construction,
maintenance and operation of all transport facilities an analysis of the environmental
impact and to provide, where necessary, for mitigating adverse impacts. In both the
proposed port loan with the IDB and the proposed National Highways Project to be
financed by the World Bank, all project components will be subject to the above
environmental policy.
Signed
Secretario de Estado de Obras Publicas y Comunicaciones, SEOPC

ANNEX 3
DOMINICAN REPUBLIC
NATIONAL HIGHWAY PROJECT
ROAD MAINTENANCE ADMINISTRATION AND PROGRAMMING
A.   Current Organization and Management of Road Maintenance
1.01  Background. Prior to Executive Decree No. 326-87 of June 18, 1987, road maintenance
in the Dominican Republic was the responsibility of two separate and entirely independent
General Directorates: the General Directorate of Road Maintenance for national roads and the
General Directorate of Rural Roads. With the Decree No. 326-87, they were merged into one,
the General Directorate of Highways and Rural Roads (DGM). This new entity was made
responsible for the maintenance of both national and rural roads, as well as related bridges,
structures and drainage works, and incorporated the former Directorate General of Rural Roads.
Since then, the staff of the two previous entities have come under the same management. The
departments, sections and units of the new DGM attend to the maintenance of both national and
local roads, although some sections belong to different lines of command.
1.02 Personnel. In June 1993, DGM had a total of 4,510 staff, at a payroll cost of DR$48.6
million (US$3.9 million), to attend to the routine and periodic maintenance of 5,050 km of
highways and 12,860 km of rural roads. Some 3,000 staff were employed in highway
maintenance at a payroll cost of DR$34 million (US$2.7 million). This represented a significant
reduction in maintenance labor costs, which had thus been brought down from 80% of total
maintenance cost in 1989 to 53% in 1993. However, in 1994, due to a reduced budget allocation
with the same manpower force, the roadworks payroll figure rose again in percentage terms,
reaching 67.5% of total maintenance cost (Graph IA). The proposed project will drastically
reverse this trend, since roadwork manpower is projected to cost US$134,000 in the year 2001
(Graph IB), for a total work force of 115 individuals (Graph IB).
1.03  Equipment Management. There are currently two Subdirectorates of Equipment and
Transportation, one based in Santo Domingo and the other in Santiago. While not clearly
defined, their functions are similar, although the Santiago branch is supervised by its counterpart
in Santo Domingo. This situation explains why in some cases the lines of command disappear,
and information flows do not provide a clearer and better basis for decision-making.
1.04 In 1994, DGM set up an Equipment Management Unit, vesting it with responsibility for
reviewing: (a) requisitions for tires and batteries, and (b) consumption of fuel and lubricants.
Although this Unit is not shown on the most recent DGM organization chart, it should be
incorporated into the Subdirectorate of Equipment and support the districts and subdistricts
(ayudantias) as far as equipment operation and maintenance is concerned. If the Unit were given
new terms of reference that included technical management of equipment, it would be of



- 54 -
invaluable assistance in implementing the Equipment Management System, in particular for repairs
and preventive maintenance.
Graph 1A: Highway Maintenance Expenses

1.05 Assignment and Distribution of Equipment. DGM equipment is distributed among
four maintenance districts, divided in their turn into 21 subdistricts. Currently, the Equipment and
Transportation Subdirectorate which is headquartered in Santo Domingo is responsible for
assigning units of maintenance equipment to the various districts, and each district for assignments
to its own subdistricts. Operation and maintenance of this equipment is the responsibility of
district and subdistrict managers. Table A2. 1 shows the composition of the present fleet, by type
of unit.
Table A 2.1
Equipment  Trucks   Water  Asphalt Motograders  Front    Light   Heavy  Tractors  Small
trucks  trucks            Loaders  Rollers  Rollers      Tractors
Nurnber of   125   21      4        24        33      21      33     4        21
1.06  Since road maintenance activities are not "programmed," in the strict sense of the word, it
is not possible to obtain a clear picture of equipment use. Neither, therefore, is it possible to give
a breakdown of the specific maintenance works for which each unit is used, or to generate reliable
data on equipment use rates and availability. In order to arrive at a satisfactory appraisal of the
state of the equipment fleet and ascertain whether the present fleet is large enough, the following
would be required: (a) an inventory of all units, including their mechanical condition; (b) a
highway and rural road maintenance program; and (c) an estimate -- based on the program -- of
the types of units needed, and the number needed of each type. The technical assistance team
financed under Loan 3350-DO to advise on maintenance management is currently working on
these three aspects of the problem.
1.07 Workshops. At the present time, DGM operates the following workshops:
(a)    Two Type A workshops in Santo Domingo (i.e. supporting the subdistrict
workshops), one for vehicles (light mechanics) and the other for equipment (heavy
mechanics);
(b)   one Type A workshop for equipment in Santiago, with similar capacity to the one
in Santo Domingo;
(c)   six Type B workshops (responsible for preventive maintenance and repair of minor
components), located in Azua, San Francisco de Macoris, Montecristi, San Pedro
de Macoris, San Juan, and Barahona; and
(d)   eight Type C workshops (only for preventive maintenance), located in Monte
Plata, Mao, Loma de Cabrera, San Crist6bal, Higuey, Cotui, Nagua, and Duverge.
1.08  The functions of each workshop depend on its classification, although the information
available on jobs in progress leaves much to be desired. Communication and coordination among
the various workshops is less than adequate, although terms of reference governing their



- 56 -
organization and functions were drawn up. Consultants are preparing a new organization and
functions for the workshops, reducing their number and potential capacity to coincide with the
gradual cutback in maintenance by force account.
1.09 Warehouses. DGM operates a central warehouse in Santo Domingo, set up as the lead
unit in charge of organizing and supervising the other warehouses and supplying them with spare
parts purchased by itself In the districts and subdistricts, DGM operates fourteen more
warehouse facilities, which hold a reduced inventory of spares that are requisitioned as necessary
for delivery to the workshops.
1.10  There is not the kind of communication between the central and satellite warehouses
which allows the stocks held in each of them to be determined at a given moment. Nor are
periodic checks made to ascertain whether inventory recorded as being in stock coincides with
actual holdings. Despite the merging of the two former General Directorates in 1987, there are
cases of duplication of facilities, making it advisable to carry out a study to determine which
should be retained. The consultants are also preparing a new warehousing plan consistent with
the staffing and equipment cutbacks referred to above.
1.11  Training. Personnel training began under the Second Road Maintenance and
Reconstruction Project (Loan 1784-DO), with the establishment of "training production units"
(UPEs) directed by Roy Jorgensen Associates, Inc., consultants. The training methodology
consisted of intensive theoretical instruction backed up by practical classes supervised and
conducted by previously trained instructors. The practical component involved maintenance of
road sections (as part of the mobile UPE) plus repair of equipment (as part of the mechanics
UPE). From 1981 to 1986, continuous financing was available for these training arrangements,
which included building and launching the Santa Caputa workshop in Sanchez. The results speak
for themselves: more than 600 individuals trained, some of them to the level of first class
mechanic; rehabilitation of 39 equipment units; and repairs on a total of 105 units.
1.12 Following the merger of the two General Directorates, training center operations were
discontinued, and the facility itself was dismantled in February 1990. As far as current problems
are concerned, which have to do mainly with equipment management, an operator training
program has been launched as part of the ongoing project, with another for mechanics to follow.
B.    Maintenance Management and Programs
2.01  Background: Resource allocations for road maintenance (routine and periodic) and
rehabilitation were adequate until approximately 1958, after which a process of steady
deterioration set in, reaching its lowest point in the early 70s. From 1976 to 1980 there was some
overall improvement, but between 1980 and 1986 overstaffing, in conjunction with the oil crisis,
which greatly reduced the country's reserves, resulted in a significant limit on the resources
available for the road sector, to the point that the maintenance battle was virtually lost (Graph 2).
2.02  Inadequate maintenance only accelerated the general degree of road deterioration, until
finally the whole network needed rehabilitation,. This was begun under Loan 2609-DO, which



- 57 -
included financing for the reconstruction of some of the most important highway sections. With
the backing of this loan -- and later that of Loan 33 50-DO, which was declared effective in
December 1992 -- the Dominican Government launched a massive road reconstruction/
rehabilitation offensive in 1987, which meant that there was once again some point to organizing
maintenance programs. By mid 1994, the policy of reconstruction or rehabilitation had returned
70% of the road system to a state of good or fair condition -- a figure projected to rise to 87% by
the end of 1997. In these circumstances, it makes very good sense to strengthen SEOPC
administratively and technically to monitor the execution of preventive maintenance tasks --
something that could not be done during earlier periods of technical assistance (1976-1987), when
the road system was markedly deteriorated.
Graph 2: Real Maintenance Expenditure / Optimal Maintenance Expenditure
14.
12

2.03  Since its current road maintenance planning and programming capability is limited, the
DGM normally relies on the Department of Pavement Management and Studies (DAEP) and the
Directorate General of Investment Planning and Programming to draw up its annual budgets and
programs, although without the kind of annually updated survey of the road system condition that
would allow realistic budgeting for necessary activities. As a result of these shortcomings, the
annual budgets prepared for the last three years have been similar in terms of quantities of work
and cost estimates -- in other words, hardly more than a hollow formality. As regards the
monitoring and evaluation of program execution, the data the districts are supposed to send in to
the DGM Planning Unit are either incomplete or nonexistent, which means it is unable to make a
comparative analysis of programmed versus actual performance and therefore, to produce any ex
post evaluation.



- 58 -
2.04  The practice until quite recently was for SEOPC itself to execute the maintenance
program. Contracting out of periodic maintenance to private sector firm's began to gain ground
under Loan 2609-DO, increased significantly under Loan 3350-DO and is currently the norm in
the periodic maintenance of paved roads (resealing, resurfacing). Routine maintenance, however,
is carried out with SEOPC personnel and equipment, although not satisfactorily or efficiently.
Senior SEOPC management has fully accepted the need for a preventive maintenance system
which safeguards the investment already made, but it is still necessary to insist on this policy with
middle management and to set up the required organization. In a move to rectify present
maintenance planning and execution arrangements, the technical assistance consultants financed
under Loan 3350-DO are introducing a maintenance management system, recommending
procedures for the contracting out of preventive (routine) maintenance, and training the SEOPC
personnel who will be planning and monitoring maintenance programs in the future. The
proposed project would finance: (i) a pilot program to foster and develop the contracting out of
routine maintenance as a first step, and of all maintenance subsequently, and (ii) technical
assistance and goods required for completion of the necessary institutional development.
2.05 Future Management and Organization: In addition to contributing substantially to the
Government's ongoing road reconstruction/rehabilitation effort, Loan 3350-DO included two
important components focused on ensuring that the benefit of that effort would be sustainable: (a)
the previously mentioned technical assistance with introduction of a road maintenance
management system (SAMC) and preparation for the contracting out of routine maintenance; and
(b) a pavement technology transfer program (periodic maintenance). Both programs began in
early 1995 and are expected to conclude in 1997, with the following results:
(a)   institutionalization, within SEOPC, of a management system that will allow
programming, monitoring and evaluation of preventive and periodic road
maintenance;
(b)   implementation of contracting maintenance activities out to private sector
enterprises, strengthening of the current practice of contracting out present
periodic maintenance (sealing and surfacing), and introducing contracting out of
routine maintenance (including patching) -- programs for which the proposed
project includes financing;
(c)   technical and managerial upgrading of firms engaged in road maintenance, through
the transfer of appropriate technology, especially in selection of materials, use of
equipment and tools, operating and construction methods, and organization and
management;
(d)   establishment of a road maintenance fund, which guarantees the availability of
funds for programmed maintenance (through special accounts earmarked for the
deposit of user charges), and administration of this fund by a new road agency --
possibly a joint organization combining government and user representatives;



- 59 -
(e)    development of an ongoing training program for DGM personnel, focused on
recognition of the profession of "road maintenance specialist" under the provisions
of the Civil Service Act.
2.06 Management System. The road maintenance management system, under implementation
in the DGM with funds provided under Loan 3350-DO, follows the general outlines indicated
above and is being introduced in the following stages:
(a)    1995-2000: Restructuring of the road maintenance organization, with special
attention to be paid to:
(i)    preparation of the road condition inventory, including traffic data;
(ii)    cataloguing of job positions, introduction of the administrative career of
road maintenance specialist and preparation of a program for institutional
reorganization of the road maintenance system. These aspects will be kept
in time with the gradual implementation of maintenance by contract, which
will begin in 1997 and will be extended to the whole network by 2001
(Table A2.2).

- 60 -
equipment operators, monitoring clerks, cost-control and accounting
personnel, etc.).
(b)   2001 and beyond: From 2001 onwards, SEOPC will be directly responsible only
for emergency and non-programmable activities, roadworks in which contractors
evince no interest, and supervisory and inspection functions. SEOPC will also
retain responsibility for planning, programming, monitoring and evaluation of
results.
2.07 Concerning equipment, workshop and training management, the action plan scheduled for
the period from 1997 to 2000 consists of: (i) removal of non-operational equipment units from the
fleet, and adjustment of fleet size and composition to adequately cover highway maintenance
(emergencies + works of no interest to contractors) and rural road maintenance; (ii) rehabilitation
of essential equipment; (iii) selection and conditioning of workshop and warehouse facilities,
chiefly those involved in preventive maintenance and minor repairs; and (iv) establishment of a
training entity and training program, with support from equipment suppliers.
2.08 Future Management and Organization Structure. Charts I and II show, respectively,
an initial proposal for DGM's management structure and the corresponding organization chart.
2.09 Resource Control. The financial resources required to ensure an acceptable level of
maintenance (routine and periodic) amount to approximately DR$160 million (US$13 million) per
year. This sum is large enough to warrant not only management and execution of the related
works programs but also the backing of decisions to ensure that those funds are spent rationally
and efficiently. In addition, the programming and budgeting for all maintenance work, whether
preventive or periodic, is approved by the Executive Branch and endorsed by the Congress via
enactment of each year's Budget Law. This means that the Office of the Inspector General
('Contralor") of the Republic has to verify -- at least through financial audits -- that budgetary
commitments have been fulfilled. Furthennore, the proposed project calls for introduction of both
technical and financial audit by independent technical and financial auditors (see main text, paras.
1.29, 2.06 and 3.07, and Annex 11.) to monitor the impact on maintenance expenditures and
activities of the proposed reforms.

ANNEX 4
DOMINICAN REPUBLIC
NATIONAL HIGHWAY PROJECT
PREVIOUS BANK EXPERIENCE IN THE TRANSPORT SECTOR
1.    The Bank's past involvement in the road transport sub-sector dates back to 1973,
when it acted as the executing agency for the UNDP-financed Highway Rehabilitation and
Administration Study. Between 1976 and 1985, three highway projects were financed
under Bank loans, totaling US$65 million, to improve the condition of some 320 kmi, or
about six percent of the highway network. Those projects were: the First Road
Maintenance and Reconstruction Project; the Emergency Road Reconstruction Project;
and, the Second Road Maintenance and Reconstruction Project.
2.    The First Road Maintenance and Reconstruction Project (Loan 1316-DO, US$5
million, 1976) provided for the reconstruction of a section of the Duarte highway
connecting the capital with Santiago, the second largest city. Other components were a
bridge rehabilitation program and support to the Road Maintenance Directorate of
SEOPC. The Project Performance Audit Report No. 3716 (Board Doc. No. Sec. M-81-
987) of 1981 indicated that project implementation generally developed as planned, but
price increases forced a reduction in the scope of the project since it took 2.5 years to
achieve congressional ratification and effectiveness of the loan.
3.    The second Bank operation was an Emergency Road Reconstruction Project
(Loan 1783-DO, US$25 million, 1980), which was approved by the Bank in the wake of
the September 1979 hurricane. It supported reconstruction of 150 km of hurricane-
damaged roads connecting agricultural areas in the northwest with Santiago and in the
southwest with Santo Domingo. Because of its emergency nature, the loan was approved
by the Bank before engineering designs were available for the project. When these were
later completed and bids were received, it became evident, even after lowering standards
to obtain cost savings, that project costs would be about 50% above those estimated at
loan approval. The Project Completion Report (December 1984) indicates that beginning
implementation of the project on the basis of incomplete engineering work was the
primary cause of later delays and cost increases, but that the project, when completed,
remained economically justified.
4.    The third road project was the Second Road Maintenance and Reconstruction
Project (Loan 1784-DO, US$35 million, 1980), amended in 1983 to provide the necessary
financing to complete the Emergency Project's road works. The amendment, resulting in
the expenditure of some of the funds originally intended for the Second Road Maintenance
and Reconstruction Project, caused, in turn, an unfinanced gap for civil works originally
included in that project. To remedy this, one of the main objectives of the Third (fourth
project) Road Reconstruction Project was to close the financing gap to complete
reconstruction of the 150 km that were not finished under the Loan 1784-DO.



- 70 -
5.   In 1986 the fourth operation, the Third Road Reconstruction Project (Loan 2609-
DO, US$35.8 million) was approved. It provided financing for: reconstruction of 150 km
of highways that were started under Loan 1784-DO, and about 60 km of other high-
priority highways; purchase of road maintenance equipment and spare parts; consulting
services for construction supervision; a transport planning study; and assistance in training
the Pavement Division staff of DGPP and in organizing workshops and stores. Generally,
the project was executed as planned and completed on December 31, 1993.
6.    The project's scope was appropriate for the country's needs but its implementation
was beset by problems which delayed its completion. Most of the roads, especially those
added to the road program in 1990, were finished in good time. However, the completion
of some reconstruction works contracted in 1981 took about six to ten years (1981-1991)
instead of four, as originally scheduled. In retrospect, the problems with these roads could
have been avoided had more attention been given during project preparation to avoid
repetition of problems experienced by the SEOPC (which hindered implementation of
preceding Bank financed projects) namely, starting project implementation with
inadequately prepared engineering and deficient planning and programming for the
execution of the road works initially included in the project. On the other hand,
engineering designs and procurement procedures prepared for the reconstruction works
that were added to the works program in 1990 improved markedly and the works were
successfully carried out within their allocated budgets, under the supervision of a
competent local consulting firm.
7.    The lessons learned confirm that to ensure successful implementation of Bank
projects it is necessary, during the project preparation stage, to emphasize the need for
uncompromising quality of engineering designs and contracting qualified contractors and
consultants. Other issues which should have been addressed concerned the Borrowers'
sense of ownership of some project components (in particular the Transport Plan) and its
commitment to support and achieve the agreed project objectives for those components by
providing the required human and financial resources and logistic support. The project
also demonstrated the benefit of maintaining an open and productive dialogue with the
Borrower, which apparently resulted in a more intensive involvement of SEOPC's higher
level staff in the execution of the project, facilitating the project's implementation and
ensuring close collaboration with the pre>ect executing agency.
8.    The Fifth Road Rehabilitation and Maintenance Project (Loan 3350-DO, US$79
million, 1991) was declared effective on December 2, 1992. The project comprises: (a)
rehabilitation of about 830 km of roads and supervision of these works; (b) a technology
transfer program on pavements, including technical assistance and civil works; (c) bridge
and workshop rehabilitation; (d) equipment rehabilitation; (e) acquisition of road signaling
materials, maintenance equipment and scales for vehicle weight control; and (f) technical
assistance for training SEOPC's maintenance staff, road maintenance management, an
urban transport study, a local construction industry study and engineering services for
road design.



- 71 -
9.    Past lessons learned have helped to successfully implement this project.
Prequalification of contractors and bidding documents were agreed at negotiations; ICB
procedures applied to all civil works of the road rehabilitation program; engineering
designs were prepared well in advance to the bidding process; and the open dialogue with
the Borrower continued. As a result, procurement is already completed; the contract for
the pavement technology transfer program (a major component) is satisfactorily
underway; compliance with loan covenants and the Action Plan is acceptable; and about
75% of the loan is already disbursed.
10.   However, the proceeds of the Loan will only reach to finance about 70% of the
length of highway rehabilitation originally programmed. The main reasons for this
reduction are cost increases for civil works, due to higher unit costs and the additional
road deterioration that took place since the project was appraised and, above all, the
upgrade of most of the works from rehabilitation to reconstruction. On the other hand, the
SEOPC rehabilitated more than 900 km in the same period with local funds, which is more
than the length finally not financed by the Loan. The technology transfer program, a
single component and the biggest contract of the project (including about 500 Km of
highway resurfacing) is being implemented as planned and within the resources allocated
in the Loan.

ANEXO 6.1
REPUBLICA DOMINICANA
PROYECTO DE CARRETERAS NACIONALES
TkRMINOS DE REFERENCIA PARA LA SUPERVISI6N DE OBRAS CIVLES
A.    Servicios Requeridos
1.    Introducci6n. Los servicios que se describen en los parrafos siguientes del
presente documento se refieren a la supervisi6n de las obras civiles de ampliaci6n,
construcci6n y reconstrucci6n de carreteras y puentes incluidas en el proyecto que se
propone. Las firmas consultoras interesadas deberan conocer todos los documentos de
licitaci6n de las obras a supervisar, asi como toda aquella informaci6n que fuese necesario
recopilar para la evaluaci6n de los requerimientos y la confecci6n de su oferta.
2.    Informes y Documentos. Todos los informes y documentos se presentarin en
formato 81/2xl 1" y los planos doblados en el mismo formato. Se seguira una secuencia
numerada. En la portada constara el titulo del proyecto, nuimero del informe y contenido.
Los informes tendran indice y paginas numeradas.
3.    Revisi6n Inicial de los Proyectos. Dado el tiempo transcurrido entre la toma de
datos, la redacci6n del proyecto base, la licitaci6n y la adjudicaci6n de las obras y el
comienzo de las mismas, la primera tarea del Consultor serk, la revisi6n, y modificaci6n en
su caso, del "proyecto de ejecuci6n" que debera realizar en el plazo de 2 meses. El
supervisor podra utilizar toda aquella informaci6n del proyecto base que considere
oportuna y la complementara hasta concluir un documento que identifique y defina
claramente la obra a ejecutar y permita al contratista la construcci6n y su programaci6n sin
vacios de definici6n. Las posibles modificaciones que se consideren y los variaciones
presupuestarias que resulten seran discutidas y aprobadas en su caso por la SEOPC,
siempre dentro del alcance inicial del proyecto base.
4.    Plan de Control. El plan de control consistira basicamente en la definici6n de una
guia de toma de datos, ensayos, medidas y comprobaciones para cada unidad y fase de
obra, estableciendo parametros minimos y criterios de aceptaci6n para el avance de la
obra, la observaci6n y paralizaci6n de esta, su aprobaci6n o su rechazo; estableciendo para
ello un conjunto de sistemas de operaci6n. Este plan sera sometido a la SEOPC para su
aprobaci6n. Tambien se establecera una colecci6n de formularios para ensayos de
laboratorio y otros controles que permitan su mejor identificaci6n, seguimiento y archivo,
definiendo dos campos: control geometrico y control de calidad. El primer campo para
verificar: (i) la concordancia de la construcci6n con las lineas generales de proyecto, y las
tolerancias asumibles; y (ii) las mediciones, y cubicaciones del contratista. El segundo
campo para verificar: (i) el cumplimiento de las especificaciones y normativa,
estableciendo al mismo tiempo frecuencia y tolerancias; y (ii) el cumplimiento de los
estandares de calidad en el proceso constructivo.



- 80 -
5.    Revisi6n de las Medidas de Mitigaci6n de Posibles Impactos. El consultor
revisari las medidas de mitigaci6n de impacto previstas en cada subproyecto considerando
los aspectos socioecon6mico y ambiental.
6.    Propuesta de Sefhalizaci6n y Balizamiento de Obra. El consultor propondra un
disefio de seftalizaci6n horizontal y vertical durante las obras que permita ejecutarlas en
suficientes condiciones de seguridad.
7.    Propuesta de Conservaci6n y Mantenimiento durante las Obras. El consultor
propondra un sistema de conservaci6n y mantenimiento durante la ejecuci6n de las obras,
teniendo en cuenta los datos basicos del proyecto, estableciendo unas condiciones minimas
de servicio y un nivel maximo de deterioro admisible e indicando las acciones a
implementar en cada caso y una estimaci6n de su costo.
8.    Acta de Replanteo. El consultor coordinara una reuni6n de la SEOPC con el
contratista y 61 mismo para dar inicio oficial a la obras, realizando una visita al lugar de las
mismas y recogiendo en un acta aquellos planteamientos que expongan las partes que
puedan afectar o no sean congruentes con la documentaci6n contractual; asimismo
recogera en el acta los acuerdos y/o acciones a realizar y anexari los informes y
documentos necesarios.
9.    Informe Inicial. El consultor redactara un informe con todas las incidencias
previas al inicio de las obras en el que incluira: (i) fechas de pago de anticipo; (ii) acta de
replanteo y programa de obra actualizado; (iii) modificaciones del proyecto base recogidas
en el proyecto de ejecuci6n, tanto de disefio como presupuestarias; (iv) medidas previstas
para la resoluci6n de los problemas encontrados al inicio de la obra; (v) evaluaci6n del
sobrecosto de la aplicaci6n de dichas medidas; y (vi) evaluaci6n de la posibilidad de
cumplimiento de las metas del proyecto .
10.   Contrataci6n de un Laboratorio Homologado. El consultor analizara la
propuesta de contrataci6n del laboratorio presentada por el contratista y confirmari que
refine las condiciones necesarias y suficientes para desarrollar eficazmente el plan de
control previsto. Asimismo, preparara las condiciones para la coordinaci6n eficaz con la
obra, plazos para los resultados de los ensayos y penalizaciones por retrasos 6 perdidas.
11.   Redisefios y Cambios. El consultor realizara todos los diseflos de partes de obra
que deban modificarse por alguna raz6n tecnica o por indicaci6n directa de la SEOPC.
Estos disefnos incluiran memoria, planos y presupuesto conciliado con el contratista y se
redactaran en el formato y como complemento del proyecto de ejecuci6n. El supervisor
completara esta etapa con la rapidez necesaria en cada caso para afectar lo menos posible
el programa de obra.
12.   Evaluaci6n de Controles. El consultor mantendra los sistemas propuestos en el
plan de control para la evaluaci6n de los resultados de los ensayos y controles de obra, de
tal forma que la emisi6n de dictamenes (valoraci6n, rechazo o aceptaci6n) se produzca de
forma automatica, con la mayor rapidez y asegurando la comunicaci6n al contratista; asi



- 81 -
evitara retenciones o paralizaciones innecesarias del ciclo productivo de partes de obra
que puedan convertirse posteriormente en causal de reclamaci6n del contratista. Todas
estas evaluaciones se archivaran junto con los resultados de los ensayos.
13.   Programaci6n. El consultor: (i) realizara un seguimiento mensual del programa
de obra, extrapolando, con rigor y con base en los avances reales y rendirnientos
promedios, la fecha de terminaci6n mas probable de la obra y mantendra puntualmente
informados a la SEOPC y al contratista del programa; (ii) mantendra reuniones con el
contratista para analizar el programa y tomar medidas implementando mas o mejores
medios en caso de existir retrasos imputables al contratista; (iii) preparara un nuevo
programa 6 una modificaci6n del existente, buscando lograr las metas previstas; y (iv)
redactara un acta de cada reuni6n. En todo caso no pasara mas de un mes sin actualizar el
programa que s6lo podra basarse en rendimniento reales o compromisos firmados por el
contratista. Los aumentos de plazo deberan someterse a la aprobaci6n de la SEOPC.
14.   Cubicaciones y Facturaci6n. El consultor tramitara, en el plazo establecido por
la SEOPC, la cubicaci6n del contratista, para lo cual acordara con el mismo el proceso a
seguir (fecha de mediciones, forma de presentaci6n, datos y calculos que anexar etc.). El
supervisor tendra en cuenta todos los aspectos contractuales, (retenci6n, devoluci6n del
anticipo, penalizaciones, baja de oferta, revisi6n de precios, etc.). La cubicaci6n
contendra la medici6n, monto y % de avance de cada unidad presupuestaria, tanto del
mes, como a origen. Los imprevistos, trabajos por administraci6n y partidas nuevas iran
en sendos capitulos adicionales. El supervisor debera anexar la cubicaci6n revisada a la
documentaci6n contractual que modifique el contrato vigente, aprobaci6n de precios
nuevos, 6rdenes de penalizaci6n, pago de impuestos e indenmizaciones, variaci6n de
retenciones o de vol(imenes y montos presupuestarios, etc. En caso de precios
provisionales y pagos por acopios se adjuntara la documentaci6n justificativa.
15.   Reclamos del Contratista. El consultor analizara de inmediato cada situaci6n
que pueda ocasionar un reclamo por el contratista, buscando la mejor soluci6n para el
proyecto dentro de los terminos contractuales. Mantendra informada a la SEOPC de los
reclamos que se presenten, adjuntando su opini6n tecnica y los efectos estimados en plazo
y calidad de obra. En general, todas las conciliaciones de reclamos y precios nuevos
debera aprobarlas la SEOPC, para lo cual el supervisor realizara las diligencias oportunas.
16.   Indicaciones al Contratista. A partir del acta de replanteo, el consultor debera
comunicar al contratista todas las 6rdenes y directrices, formatos de formularios, sistemas
de trabajo, coordinaci6n en obra, reuniones y consideraciones del programa de obra a
seguir, etc. que entienda sean necesarios para supervisar eficazmente la obra y asegurar su
calidad.
17.   Informe Mensual. Basicamente el informe mensual servirA de soporte al analisis
del avance de obra y la situaci6n de los problemas surgidos y su posible resoluci6n, asi
como a las variaciones de proyecto incorporadas. No se adjuntaran con el informe



- 82 -
documentos de trabajo rutinario, como formularios de ensayos, listados topograficos y

18.   Informes Especiales. El consultor realizara los informes especificos que le sean

19.   Pianos "As Built". Al finalizar las obras, el consultor redactara un proyecto "as

20.   Cubicaci6n de Liquidaci6n. El consultor emitira una cubicaci6n final de obra

22.   Informe Final. El consultor emitira un informe final recogiendo el acta de

B.    Estructura Operacional
